Validity of Settlement after Judicial Ruling: Conditions and Limits
Conditions for settlement after/before judicial ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on when a settlement for a wound is valid after a qadi's ruling, based on amount, timing, and type.

Core Idea
A settlement exceeding the qadi's diya ruling is void unless the increase is in a different type and immediate.

When a qadi has already ruled a specific diya for a wound, can the parties settle for a different amount? The passage from al-Zahiriyya details several conditions: after or before ruling, more or less than the ruling, same or different type of property. This lesson unpacks those rules.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish after ruling vs. before ruling
The rules differ depending on whether the settlement occurs after the qadi's ruling (or mutual consent) or before any ruling.
2
After ruling: settlement for more than the ruled amount
If settlement is for more than the diya amount ruled by the qadi, it is generally void. Exception: if the extra amount is in a different type (e.g., camels instead of dirhams) and the transaction is hand-to-hand (immediate), it may be valid.
Example: qadi ruled 10,000, settlement for 15,000 – void. But if qadi ruled 100 camels, settlement for 150 specific camels hand-to-hand – valid.
3
After ruling: settlement for less than the ruled amount
If settlement is for less than the diya, it is valid regardless of whether payment is immediate or deferred.
This encourages reconciliation at a lower amount.
4
Before ruling: settlement for more than diya
If settlement occurs before any qadi's ruling, and the settled amount is more than the standard diya (e.g., 100 camels or equivalent), the settlement is void.
Because it would involve riba or injustice to the victim's heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن أبي يوسف في جامع : إذا صالح الشاج من موضحة الخطأ على خمسمائة درهم ثم مات منها يحط عن العاقلة الثلث وبطل الصلح ويرجع الشاج بما دفع. وفي الكبرى : وهذا الجواب على قولهما خاصة، أما على قول أبي حنيفة فالصلح والعفو عن الشجة لا يتناول ما يحدث منها، فإذا مات المشجوج ها هنا صار وجود الصلح كعدمه عنده، ولو انعدم الصلح عنده فالدية على عاقلة الشاج كذا هنا. وفي الظهيرية : وإن وقع الصلح على خمسة عشر ألفاً بعد قضاء القاضي بعشرة آلاف فهذا الصلح باطل لما فيه من الزيادة على الدية، وإن كان مائة المقضي به من الإبل فاصطلحا على مائة وخمسين إن وقع الصلح نسيئة لا شك أنه لا يجوز، وإن كان يداً بيد إن كان الإبل بأعيانها ثم اصطلحوا على مائة وخمسين من الإبل بأعيانها كان ذلك جائزاً هذا إذا وقع الصلح على أكثر من النوع الذي وقع به القضاء، أما إذا وقع الصلح على أقل مما وقع به القضاء فإنه يجوز حالاً ونسيئة، وإذا اصطلحا على خلاف جنس ما وقع به القضاء وقد صالحه على أكثر مما قضى به فإنه يجوز. هذا الذي ذكرنا إذا اصطلحا بعد القضاء أو الرضا، أما إذا اصطلحا قبل القضاء إن كان المصالح عليه أكثر من الدية فإنه لا يجوز. ابن سماعة عن محمد في رجل جرحه رجلان جراحة عمداً فقضى بالقصاص على أحدهما ثم مات من الجراحتين قال : لورثته أن يقتلوا الآخر، ولو جرحه رجل
And from Abu Yusuf in al-Jami': If the wounder settles for an accidental head wound for five hundred dirhams, then the victim dies from it, one-third is deducted from the 'aqila, the settlement is void, and the wounder takes back what he paid. In al-Kubra: This answer is specifically according to the two Sahibayn. As for Abu Hanifa's view, settlement and forgiveness for the wound do not cover what results from it. So when the wounded dies here, the existence of the settlement is like its non-existence according to him. If settlement ceased according to him, then the diya is upon the 'aqila of the wounder. Likewise here. In al-Zahiriyya: If settlement occurred on fifteen thousand after the qadi's ruling of ten thousand, this settlement is void because of the increase over the diya. If the ruled amount was one hundred camels, and they settled on one hundred fifty, if the settlement was deferred, there is no doubt it is not permissible. If it was hand-to-hand, and the camels were specific, then they settled on one hundred fifty specific camels, that is permissible. This is if the settlement is for more than the type that was ruled. As for if the settlement is for less than what was ruled, it is permissible immediately or deferred. If they settle on a different type from what was ruled, and he settled for more than what was ruled, it is permissible. What we have mentioned is if they settle after the ruling or consent. As for if they settle before the ruling, if the settled amount is more than the diya, it is not permissible. Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: if two men wounded a man intentionally, and qisas was decreed against one, then he died from the two wounds, he said: his heirs may kill the other.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage brings together multiple fatwas from Hanafi masters. First, from Abu Yusuf: if a wounder settles for an accidental wound, then the victim dies, the settlement is partially void (only one-third deducted from 'aqila, per Sahibayn). According to Abu Hanifa, the settlement does not cover future death, so if death occurs, the settlement is void entirely, and the full diya is on the 'aqila. Then from al-Zahiriyya: if a settlement exceeds the qadi's ruling, its validity depends on timing and type. If after ruling, settlement for more than the diya is void; if for less, it is valid. If the type differs, increase may be allowed in certain conditions (e.g., specific camels hand-to-hand). Before ruling, settlement for more than diya is void. Finally, from Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: if two men intentionally wound, and qisas is decreed against one, but the victim dies from both wounds, the heirs may kill the other (the one not decreed against). This shows that death from combined wounds can still trigger qisas against the second.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): Settlement or compromise between parties.
يداً بيد (yadan bi yad): Hand-to-hand, immediate exchange in a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a qadi's ruling of 100 camels, the parties settle for 150 camels to be paid immediately with specific camels. Is this valid?
A) Yes, because it is hand-to-hand and the camels are specific.
B) No, because it exceeds the ruled amount.
C) Yes, because the parties agreed.
D) No, because it must be deferred.

Key Takeaway
A settlement after a qadi's ruling is valid if for less than the ruling, or if for more but immediate and in a different type; before ruling, any increase over diya is void.

Two Wounders and Qisas: Heirs' Right to Execute the Other
Fatwa on two wounders and qisas after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when two people intentionally wound someone and qisas is decreed against only one, but the victim dies from both wounds, the heirs may kill the other wounder.

Core Idea
Death from combined wounds of two people can trigger qisas against the one not yet punished.

What happens when two people intentionally wound a victim, only one is sentenced to qisas, but the victim dies from the combination of both wounds? The Hanafi fatwa from Ibn Sama'a clarifies that the heirs may still execute the other wounder. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial scenario
Two men intentionally wound a third person. The qadi decrees qisas (retaliatory punishment) against only one of them. The victim then dies from the combined effect of both wounds.
2
Identify the legal issue
Does the death from the combined wounds give the heirs the right to seek qisas against the other wounder, given that qisas was already decreed against one?
The issue is whether the death is attributable to both or only the one already punished.
3
State the ruling from Muhammad via Ibn Sama'a
Muhammad al-Shaybani says: the heirs may kill the other (the one not decreed against). This implies that the death is considered as caused by both wounders jointly.
This is a fatwa from Muhammad, recorded by Ibn Sama'a.
4
Apply the principle of joint causation
When two separate wounds contribute to death, both wounders are liable for qisas unless one has already been executed. The death activates the right against the remaining wounder.
This is analogous to joint liability in homicide.

Source Text

Certainty of Causation and Consent in Sulh
Ruling on settlement when cause of death is uncertain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sulh (settlement) in cases of uncertain causation and apply them to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Sulh in homicide is binding only when the cause of death is certain; if uncertain, all heirs must consent.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a complex scenario where a girl dies from either an arrow shot or her father's slap. Abu Salama rules on the validity of a settlement between the father and the shooter, distinguishing between certain and uncertain causation. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi position on the importance of knowledge and consent in blood money settlements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct fact patterns: one where the shooter's arrow is the certain cause (the girl died from the wound), and another where an alternative cause (father's slap) exists and it is unknown which caused death.
2
Determine the condition for sulh validity in each scenario
In scenario 1, the settlement is valid if the father knew the shooter caused the death. In scenario 2, the settlement is valid only if all other heirs (besides the father) consented to the settlement.
3
Explain the rationale behind the requirement of all heirs' consent
When the cause is uncertain, the father may be partially responsible; thus he cannot unilaterally waive the potential qisas claim that other heirs might have against him. Their consent ensures their rights are not violated.
4
Trace the legal principle: certainty of causation
The ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that rights to blood compensation (diyat) require certainty; doubt affects enforceability of settlements.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a similar case where a person dies in a group incident with unclear cause, state whether a settlement would be valid based on the principles derived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسئل أبو سلمة عن جماعة كانوا يرمون على كل كلب عقور فأخطأ واحد منهم فأصاب صغيرة فماتت وعرف أن هذا فلان ولكن لم يشهد أحد أنه رماه فلان فصالح صاحب السهم على كرم ثم طلب المصالح رد الصلح قال : إن كان يعلم أن المصالح هو الذي جرحها وأن الصبية ماتت من تلك الجراحة فالصلح ماض، فإن علم أن الجارح صاحب السهم ولكن استغاثت الصغيرة بأبيها فلطمها أبوها فسقطت وماتت ولم يدر أنها ماتت من اللطمة أو من الرمي قال : فإن كان الصلح من الأب بإذن سائر الورثة فالصلح جائز والبدل لسائر الورثة ولا ميراث للأب، وإن كان الميراث بغير إذنهم فالصلح باطل
Abu Salama was asked about a group who were shooting at a vicious dog, but one of them missed and hit a young girl, and she died. He knew that this was so-and-so, but no one testified that so-and-so shot her. He (the father) settled with the owner of the arrow for a generous amount, then the one who settled wanted to revoke the settlement. He said: If he knew that the one who settled was the one who wounded her and that the girl died from that wound, the settlement is valid. If he knew that the wounder was the owner of the arrow, but the girl cried out to her father, and her father slapped her, and she fell and died, and it is not known whether she died from the slap or from the shot, he said: If the settlement was made by the father with the permission of all the other heirs, the settlement is valid and the compensation goes to the other heirs, and the father inherits nothing. If the settlement was without their permission, the settlement is invalid.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This is a response from Abu Salama (a Hanafi jurist) on a complex case of uncertain causation. A group is shooting at a rabid dog. One misses and hits a girl, who dies. The father knows the shooter but has no witness. He settles with the shooter for a generous sum, then later wants to revoke. The ruling: If it is certain that the shooter caused the death, the settlement is binding. But if there is another possible cause (the father's slap), and the cause is uncertain, then the settlement is only valid if all heirs (besides the father) consent, because the father cannot act alone if he might be partially responsible. If he did not have permission, the settlement is void. This shows the importance of certainty in causation and the need for unanimity among heirs when the cause is doubtful.

Key Terms
كلب عقور (kalb 'aqūr): A vicious or rabid dog that poses danger.
صلح (ṣulḥ): Compromise settlement for the blood claim, waiving qisas for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a girl dies from either a shot or her father's slap, when is the settlement valid?
A) Always, because the father is the guardian.
B) Only if all other heirs consent.
C) Only if the shooter confesses.
D) It is never valid.

Key Takeaway
A settlement (sulh) in homicide is only valid if the cause of death is certain, or if the cause is uncertain, all heirs must consent to protect each individual's right to blood compensation.

Return of Settlement When Injury Heals Completely
Ruling on returning settlement when injury heals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a settlement for injury is returned if the injury heals completely, and distinguish between full recovery and residual weakness.

Core Idea
Compensation is for harm; if harm disappears, the compensation must be returned.

This lesson discusses a subtle point in Hanafi fiqh: when a person settles with the victim or guardian for an injury, and then the injury heals completely (e.g., tooth grows back, bone mends), does the settlement remain valid? The Hanafi masters Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the settlement must be returned, because the compensation was for the harm, and the harm no longer exists. This ruling, transmitted in the Nawadir of Hisham, demonstrates the importance of linking compensation to actual damage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Parties
Read the passage to identify the injuries (extracted child's tooth, shaved woman's head, broken hand) and the parties (culprit, victim/guardian, settlement for dirhams).
The key fact: the injury later heals fully (tooth regrows, hair regrows, bone sets correctly).
2
State the Ruling
Abu Hanifa rules that the settlement money must be returned. Muhammad agrees. This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is from the Nawadir, but considered reliable.
3
Identify the Condition for Return
The condition is that the injury heals to its original state without permanent defect. If there is residual weakness, an expert examines it.
The passage gives the exception: if the hand becomes weak, the settlement may not be fully returned.
4
Understand the Rational Analogy
The principle: compensation is for harm. If the harm is undone, the compensation becomes unjust and is returned. This is analogous to a sale becoming void if the subject matter is lost.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the purpose of diyat.
5
Apply the Ruling to Similar Cases
The same ruling applies to any bodily injury that heals completely after a settlement. If healing is incomplete, an assessment is made.
Practical implication: the settlement is not automatically final; it depends on the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نوادر هشام قال: سألت محمداً عند قلع سن صبي أو حلق رأس امرأة فصالح الجاني أبا الصبي والمرأة على دراهم ونبت الشعر أو السن فأخبر أن أبا حنيفة يرد الدراهم قال : وكذلك أقول، وكذلك قول محمد قال : وكذلك إن كان هذا كسر يده فصالحه عنها ثم جبرت وصحت؟ قال : نم . قلت : فإن زعم صاحب اليد أن يده قد ضعفت وليست كما كانت قال : أمر من ينظر إليها فإنه لا يكاد يخفى.
In Nawadir Hisham: He said: I asked Muhammad regarding the extraction of a child's tooth or the shaving of a woman's head; the culprit settled with the child's father and the woman for dirhams, then the hair or tooth grew back. He informed that Abu Hanifa returns the dirhams. He said: And I (Muhammad) say the same. And the same is the saying of Muhammad. He said: Similarly, if a man's hand was broken, and he settled for it, then it healed and became sound? He said: Yes. I said: If the owner of the hand claims that his hand has become weak and not as it was, he said: Appoint someone to look at it; it will hardly be hidden.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage from the Nawadir (rare transmissions) of Hisham presents a ruling from Muhammad al-Shaybani in agreement with Abu Hanifa. When a child's tooth is knocked out or a woman's hair is shaved off (both are injuries that have fixed diya or arbitration), and the culprit settles with the guardian for a sum, but then the tooth or hair grows back (restoring the original state), Abu Hanifa rules that the settlement money must be returned to the culprit because the harm has been undone. Muhammad agrees. The same rule applies if a broken bone heals properly. However, if the victim claims the limb has permanent weakness, an expert should examine it, and the truth will be apparent. This shows that if the injury completely reverses, the settlement is nullified as there is no remaining harm to compensate.

Key Terms
نبت (nabata): Regrew or was restored to its original state.
جبرت (jabarat): Healed properly without defect.
نوادر هشام (Nawadir Hisham): A collection of rare transmissions (nawadir) by Hisham ibn Ubaydillah al-Razi from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when a child's knocked-out tooth grows back after the culprit settled with the father?
A) The settlement stands as is.
B) The culprit must pay additional compensation.
C) The settlement money is returned.
D) The case is referred to a judge.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi settlement for bodily injury is conditional: if the injury heals completely, the compensation must be returned, as it is tied to the existence of harm.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Liability for Shooting a Slave Who is Later Emancipated
Explaining the ruling and proof for the shooter's liability when a wounded slave is freed before death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the shooter's liability when a wounded slave is freed before death, explain the distinction from cutting, and evaluate Zufar's opposing view.

Core Idea
Liability for killing a slave is determined by his status at the time of the act, not at death.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced case from the Hanafi school on diyah (blood money). The scenario: a shooter wounds a slave, and before the wound kills, the master emancipates the slave. When the slave dies, what is the shooter liable for? Abu Hanifa rules that the shooter pays the slave's value, not the full diyah of a free person. This ruling hinges on a key usul principle: the legal consequence of an act is determined by the status at the time of the act, even if the outcome materializes later. The passage also addresses an objection from Zufar and distinguishes this case from similar ones involving cutting or apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the Hanafi ruling
The passage opens with the ruling: 'And the value is due with his emancipation.' Understand that the shooter must pay the market value of the slave to the owner, not full blood money. Note that the victim was a slave at the time of the shooting.
The key is the time of the act (shooting) vs. time of death.
2
Understand the proof (qiyas) by analogy with cutting
The author draws an analogy to a case where a slave's hand is cut, then he is freed and dies. In that case, only compensation for the hand (arsh) is due, because the cutting is an immediate destruction of a part. The author contrasts this with shooting, which is not immediate destruction; it only becomes lethal upon impact.
Shooting is not an immediate destruction; liability attaches at the time of the act, not at impact.
3
Analyze the objection from Zufar and the Hanafi response
Zufar argues that shooting only becomes a cause at the moment of impact, which occurs after emancipation, so the shooter should pay full diyah for a free person. Abu Hanifa responds that the shooting is the cause from the start; the impact merely actualizes it. Thus, the end (death) does not override the beginning (shooting while a slave).
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa.
4
Distinguish from the apostasy analogy
The passage ends by distinguishing this case from a previous one involving apostasy. In apostasy, the slave's inviolability is destroyed, so the shooter is like a creditor. Here, emancipation confirms inviolability, so the crime remains valid.
Emancipation does not nullify the crime; it only changes the ownership of the right to compensation.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider: A slave is shot, then freed, then dies. The shooter must pay the slave's value to the former owner. If the slave had died before emancipation, the shooter would also pay the slave's value. The difference would be if the slave was cut—then only arsh for the hand.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is significant: value of slave vs. full diyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقيمة بعتقه . للسراية ألا ترى أن من قطع يد عبد ثم عتقه مولاه ثم مات منه لا يجب عليه إلا أرش اليد النقصان الذي نقصه القطع إلى العتق وهو بنفس الرمي فصار جانياً عليه لأن النقصان. ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله أن الرمي يصير قاتلاً له من وقت الرمي وهو مملوك في تلك الحالة بخلاف القطع والجرح لأن كل واحد منهما إتلاف لبعض المحل والإتلاف يوجب الضمان للمولى لأنه ورد على محل مملوك له ، ثم إذا سرى لا يوجب شيئاً لأنه لو أوجب شيئاً لوجب للعبد لا للمولى لانقطاع حق المولى عنه وظهور حقه فيه فيصير النهاية مخافة للبداية فصار ذلك كتبدل المحل، وعند تبدل المحل لا تتبدل السراية فكذا هنا. أما الرمي فقبل الإصابة به ليس بإتلاف شيء منه لأنه لا أثر له في المحل وإنما قلت فيه الرغبات فلا يجب فيه المضان قبل الاتصال بالمحل، وعند الاتصال بالمحل يستند الوجوب إلى وقت الانعقاد فلا تخالف النهاية البداية فتجب قيمته للمولى. وقال زفر رحمه الله : عليه الدية لأن الرمي إنما صار علة عند الإصابة إذ الاتلاف لا علة يصير من غير تلف يتصل به ووقت التلف المتلف حر فتجب ديته، وأبو يوسف مع أبي حنيفة فيه. والفرق له هذا بین وبين ما تقدم من الارتداد أنه اعترض على الرمي ما يوجب عصمة المحل فيما تقدم فحصل ذلك بمنزلة الإبراء، أما هنا اعترض على الرمي بما يؤكد عصمة المحل وهو الإعتاق فلا تبطل به الجناية .
And the value is due with his emancipation. Due to transmission: Do you not see that if someone cuts off the hand of a slave, then his master emancipates him, then he dies from it, nothing is obligatory upon him except the compensation for the hand—the deficiency which the cutting caused up to the emancipation, and he (the shooter) is [treated] the same as the shooter, so he becomes a transgressor against him because of the deficiency. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) says: The shooting makes him a killer from the time of the shooting, and he is a slave at that time, unlike cutting and wounding because each one of them is destruction of part of the subject and destruction necessitates liability to the owner because it occurred on property owned by him, then if it spreads it does not necessitate anything because if it necessitated something, it would be for the slave, not the owner, due to the termination of the owner's right over him and the emergence of his (the slave's) right in it, so the end becomes contradictory to the beginning, so that becomes like a change of the subject, and when the subject changes, the transmission does not change, so here too. As for shooting, before hitting it is not destruction of anything of it because it has no effect on the subject, rather [؟] desires diminish, so no liability is required before contact with the subject, and upon contact with the subject, the obligation refers back to the time of the act, so the end does not contradict the beginning, so its value is required for the owner. Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) said: He owes the blood money because shooting becomes a cause only upon hitting, since destruction cannot become a cause without connected destruction, and the time of destruction the destroyer is free, so his blood money is required. Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa on this. The distinction between this and what preceded about apostasy is that in the previous case, there intervened with the shooting something that necessitates the inviolability of the subject, so that becomes like a discharge; here, there intervened with the shooting something that confirms the inviolability of the subject, which is emancipation, so the crime is not invalidated by it.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute. The ruling is about a scenario where a shooter wounds a slave, and before the wound kills the slave, the master emancipates him. The question is: if the slave then dies from the wound, what is the shooter liable for? According to Abu Hanifa, the shooter is liable for the full value of the slave as a slave (because at the moment of shooting, the victim was a slave), not the higher blood money due for a free person. Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa. Zufar says the shooter owes the full blood money of a free person because the killing is only realized when death occurs, and at that point the victim is free, so full diyah is due. The author refutes Zufar by distinguishing shooting from cutting or wounding: cutting is an immediate destruction of a part, which creates an immediate liability to the owner; shooting, however, is not an immediate destruction—it has no effect until it hits. Therefore, when the victim is freed before the shot hits, the shooter's liability is determined by the time of the shooting when the victim was still a slave. The analogy with apostasy is rejected because apostasy destroys inviolability, while emancipation confirms it.

Key Terms
القيمة بعتقه (al-qīmah bi'atqihi): The value of the slave due at the time of his emancipation (the shooter must pay that value because the victim was a slave at the time of shooting).
السراية (al-sirāyah): The transmission or spread of an injury causing death; here it refers to the wound spreading to cause death.
أرش اليد (arsh al-yad): The compensation for the hand (a specific amount less than full diyah for a limb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person shoots a slave, and the master frees the slave before the shot hits, and then the slave dies from the wound, what is the shooter liable for?
A) Full blood money of a free person
B) The value of the slave at the time of shooting
C) Only the compensation for the wound (arsh)
D) Nothing, because the slave became free

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for killing a slave is determined by the victim's status at the time of the injurious act, not at death.

Diyat — Blood Money

The Eight Aspects of Diyah: A Hanafi Framework
Prefatory material on the book of blood money: its evidence, linguistic/legal meaning, cause, benefit, pillar, condition, and ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight aspects of diyah and explain each one, including the Hanafi position on dhimmi blood money.

Core Idea
Diyah is compensation for mistaken killing; its eight aspects form the backbone of Hanafi blood money law.

This passage from Bahar al-Raiq introduces Kitab al-Diyat systematically. It provides an eight-part framework covering evidence, meaning, cause, benefit, pillar, condition, ruling, and a key dispute with al-Shafi'i. Mastering these eight aspects will give you a comprehensive understanding of the diyah ruling in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight aspects
From the text, extract the eight enumerated aspects: evidence, linguistic meaning, legal meaning, cause, benefit, pillar, condition, ruling.
Memorize these as the standard Hanafi introductory framework for any topic.
2
Analyze the evidence and meanings
The primary evidence is Quran 4:92. The linguistic meaning is 'giving the substitute for the soul'. The legal meaning is that diyah is a technical term for life compensation, distinct from arsh (wounds).
Note the distinction between diyah (life) and arsh (limbs).
3
Understand cause and benefit
The cause of diyah is mistake (khatā’). The benefit is preventing corruption and appeasing the victim's family, thereby avoiding retaliation.
Mistake is a key element; intentional killing may lead to qisas, not diyah.
4
Determine pillar and condition
The pillar (rukn) is the act of payment. The condition is that the victim has inviolable blood (ʿiṣmat al-dam), which includes Muslims, dhimmis, and musta'min.
If the victim lacks inviolability (e.g., a harbi who did not convert), no diyah is owed.
5
Examine ruling and dispute
The ruling is that diyah expiates the sin of negligence. The Hanafi view is that diyah for dhimmis equals that of Muslims. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, giving reduced amounts. The Hanafi proof includes a hadith and caliphal practice.
The dispute is resolved by Hanafi tarjih: 'the correct is our view'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات
قال هذا الاسم.
في العناية : ذكر الديات بعد الجنايات ظاهر المناسبة لما أن الدية أحد جنابة موجبي في الآدمي صيانة له عن القصاص لكن القصاص أشد جناية فلذا قدمه. والكلام فيها من وجوه : الأول في دليل مشروعيتها، والثاني في معناها لغة والثالث في معناها عند الفقهاء، والرابع في سبب وجوبها والخامس في فائدتها والسادس في ركنها، والسابع في شرطها، والثامن في حكمها. أما دليل المشروعية فقوله تعالى ومن قتل مؤمناً خطأ فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة ودية مسلمة إلى أهله ﴾ [النساء : ۹۲] الآية. وأما معناها في اللغة فالدية مصدر ودي القاتل المقتول أعطى ديته وأعطى لوليه المال الذي هو بدل النفس، ثم قيل لذلك المال الدية تسمية بالمصدر؛ كذا في المغرب قال في القاموس : الدية حق للقتيل جمعها ديات. وفي الصحاح : وديت القتيل أديه دية إذا أعطيت ديته وأما معناها شرعاً فالدية عبارة عما يؤدي وقد صار علماً على بدل النفوس دون غيرها وهو الأرش. وأما سبب وجوبها فالخطأ فإن الآدمي لما خلق في الأصل معصوم النفس محقون الدم مضموناً عن الهدر فيجب صون حقه عن البطلان . وأما الخامس وهو فائدتها فهو دفع الفساد وإطفاء نار ولي المقتول. وأما ركنها فهو الأداء والإيتاء. وأما شرط وجوبها فكون المقتول معصوم الدم متقوماً بعصمة الدار ومنعة الإسلام حتى لو أسلم الحربي في دار الحرب ولم يهاجر إلينا فقتل لا تجب الدية. وأما حكمها فتمحيض ذنب التقصير بالتفكير وفي المبسوط : يحتاج إلى بيان كيفية وجوب الدية وكيفية مقدارها . أما كيفية وجوب الدية ففي نفس الحر تجب دية كاملة يستوي فيها الصغير والكبير، والوضيع والشريف، والمسلم والذمي. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : دية اليهودي والنصراني أربعة آلاف درهم، وفي المجوس ثمانمائة، والصحيح قولنا لما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قضى بدية المستأمنين اللذين قتلهما عمرو بن أبي أمية كدية حرين مسلمين. وعن الزهري أنه قال : قضى أبو بكر وعمر في دية الذمي بمثل دية المسلم. ولأنهما يستويان في العصمة والحرية ولهذا قال علي رضي الله عنه : إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دماؤهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا ونقص
Book of Blood Money. He said this name. In al-Inayah: Mentioning blood money (diyat) after injuries (jinayat) is clearly appropriate because blood money is one of the two obligations for killing in a human being, preserving him from retaliation; but retaliation is a greater injury, so it was presented first. The discussion about it has eight aspects: first, the evidence for its legitimacy; second, its linguistic meaning; third, its meaning according to jurists; fourth, the cause of its obligation; fifth, its benefit; sixth, its pillar; seventh, its condition; eighth, its ruling. As for the evidence of legitimacy, it is His saying (Exalted): 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then (he must) free a believing slave and pay blood money to his family' [4:92] ayah. As for its linguistic meaning, diyah is a verbal noun: wada al-qatil al-maqtul (he gave his blood money) and he gave to his heir the money which is the substitute for the soul, then that money is called diyah as a denominative from the verbal noun; like that in al-Maghrib. He said in al-Qamus: Diyah is a right for the killed, its plural is diyat. And in al-Sihah: Wadaytu al-qatil adihi diyah if you gave his blood money. As for its meaning legally, diyah is an expression for what is paid and has become a technical term for the substitute for lives, not other than it, and it is al-Arsh. As for the cause of its obligation, it is mistake; since a human being is originally created with inviolable life and protected blood, guaranteed against waste, so his right must be protected from being voided. As for the fifth, its benefit, it is to repel corruption and extinguish the fire of the heir of the killed. As for its pillar, it is payment and giving. As for the condition of its obligation, it is that the killed person have inviolable life and be valued by the inviolability of the abode and the protection of Islam; so if the Harbi becomes Muslim in the Abode of War and does not migrate to us and is killed, no blood money is obligatory. As for its ruling, it is the purification of the sin of negligence through reflection (or atonement). And in al-Mabsut: There is need to explain the modality of the obligation of blood money and its amount. As for the modality: for a free person, full blood money is obligatory, equal for small and big, lowly and noble, Muslim and dhimmī. And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The blood money of a Jew and Christian is four thousand dirhams, and for a Zoroastrian eight hundred. The correct is our view, based on what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) decreed the blood money of the two protected persons (musta'minayn) whom Amr ibn Umayyah killed as equal to the blood money of two free Muslims. And from al-Zuhri that he said: Abu Bakr and Umar decreed concerning the blood money of a dhimmī the same as the blood money of a Muslim. And because they are equal in inviolability and freedom, and for this reason Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'They only paid the jizyah so that their blood would be like our blood and their property like our property.' And (the text ends) 'and a decrease...'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This is the opening of the Book of Blood Money (Kitab al-Diyat) from Bahar al-Raiq. The author cites from al-Inayah to justify the ordering: diyat is placed after jinayat because retaliation (qisas) is a more severe topic. He then enumerates eight aspects of diyah: evidence (Quran 4:92), linguistic meaning (from wada, to pay blood money), legal meaning (a substitute for life), cause of obligation (mistake), benefit (preventing retaliation and calming families), pillar (payment), condition (the victim must be a person with inviolable blood), and ruling (expiation of sin). The passage also includes an inter-school dispute with al-Shafi'i regarding the amount of blood money for non-Muslims. The Hanafi position, supported by hadith and practice of the caliphs, is that the blood money for dhimmis is the same as for Muslims (full diyah), while al-Shafi'i distinguishes based on religion. The Hanafi view is argued using the hadith of the musta'minan and reports from Abu Bakr, Umar, and Ali.

Key Terms
الدية (al-diyah): The monetary compensation paid to the heirs of a person killed by mistake or unintentionally.
الأرش (al-arsh): Compensation for injuries less than death; sometimes used synonymously with diyah but technically for wounds.
عصمة الدم (ʿiṣmat al-dam): Inviolability of blood; the status that makes killing unlawful and requires compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Half of the blood money of a Muslim
B) The same as a Muslim: full diyah
C) 4000 dirhams
D) 800 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that diyah is equal for all free persons with inviolable blood, regardless of religion, based on prophetic and caliphal practice.

Definition and Ruling of Diyat: Evidence, Equality, and the Dhimmi Dispute
Introduction to diyat with definition, evidence, and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define diya (diyah), state its evidence from Quran 4:92, and explain why the Hanafi school holds that the diya of a dhimmi equals that of a Muslim.

Core Idea
Diya is wajib compensation for accidental killing, equal for all free persons including dhimmis.

This opening passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out the foundations of diyat (blood money). The author organizes the discussion into eight aspects, from evidence to ruling. We will focus on the definition, the Quranic proof, and the controversial ruling that a dhimmi's diya is identical to a Muslim's—a position supported by prophetic practice and early Caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight aspects of diyat
The author lists eight aspects: evidence, linguistic meaning, legal meaning, cause, benefit, pillar, condition, ruling. Scan the passage to see how each is briefly addressed.
This parses the structure of the discussion.
2
Define diya linguistically and legally
Linguistically, diya is a verbal noun meaning the payment given in place of a slain person. Legally, it is a proper name for compensation for life (not injuries, which is arsh). Note the distinction from other forms of compensation.
Hanafi usage: diya for life, arsh for injuries.
3
Examine the evidence from Quran 4:92
The verse commands freeing a slave and paying 'diyatun musallamatun' (a delivered diya) for accidental killing. The imperative indicates wujub. Identify the phrase and how it establishes the obligation.
The dalil is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalala regarding whether it implies full diya for all free persons.
4
Analyze the dispute on dhimmi diya
The text states the Hanafi position: full diya for dhimmi equals Muslim. Then presents al-Shafi'i's view (4,000/800 dirhams). The Hanafi evidence includes a hadith about musta'minin, practice of Abu Bakr and Umar, and Ali's statement. Determine how the Hanafis argue from equality of inviolability and freedom.
The passage cuts off; the full Hanafi rebuttal is not given here, but the key proofs are mentioned.

The Book of Diyat. He said: This name. In al-'Inayah: Mentioning diyat after jinayat is clearly appropriate because the diya is one of the consequences of aggression against a human being, to protect him from retaliation, but retaliation is a greater aggression, so it was mentioned first. The discussion about it has eight aspects: first, the evidence of its legislation; second, its linguistic meaning; third, its meaning according to jurists; fourth, the cause of its obligation; fifth, its benefit; sixth, its pillar; seventh, its condition; eighth, its ruling. As for the evidence of legislation, it is the saying of Allah: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing slave and a compensation (diya) handed over to his family' (An-Nisa 4:92). As for its linguistic meaning: ad-diyah is a verbal noun from wada al-qatilu al-maqtula a'ta diyatahu, and he gave to his heir the wealth that is the substitute for the soul, then that wealth is called ad-diyah by naming the verbal noun. Likewise in al-Maghrib. He said in al-Qamoos: ad-diyah is the right of the slain, its plural is diyat. And in as-Sihah: wadaitu al-qatila adeethu diyatan if I gave his diya. As for its meaning legally: ad-diyah is an expression for what is paid, and it has become a proper name for the substitute for lives, not for other things, and that is al-arsh. As for the cause of its obligation: it is error, because the human being is originally created with inviolable life, protected blood, guaranteed against being wasted, so his right must be preserved from nullification. As for the fifth, its benefit: it is to prevent corruption and to extinguish the fire of the slain's heir. As for its pillar: it is payment and giving. As for the condition of its obligation: that the slain be of inviolable blood, valuable by the inviolability of the abode and the protection of Islam, so if a war captive becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and does not migrate to us, and is killed, diya is not obligatory. As for its ruling: it is a purification of the sin of negligence by expiation. In al-Mabsut: there is need to explain the manner of obligation of diya and the manner of its amount. As for the manner of obligation of diya: for the free person a complete diya is obligatory, equal for young and old, low and high, Muslim and dhimmi. And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: the diya of a Jew and a Christian is four thousand dirhams, and of a Magian eight hundred. The correct is our statement because it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged the diya of the two musta'mins whom Amr ibn Umayya killed as the diya of two free Muslims. And from al-Zuhri that he said: Abu Bakr and Umar judged the diya of a dhimmi like the diya of a Muslim. And because they are equal in inviolability and freedom, and for this Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: they only paid jizya so that their blood would be like our blood and their wealth like our wealth. And (the text) is incomplete.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Kitab al-Diyat from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It explains the rationale for placing diyat after jinayat: diya is one of the two consequences (along with qisas) of aggression against a person, but qisas is more severe so it was presented first. The author, al-Marghinani, then outlines eight aspects of diyat: evidence, linguistic meaning, legal meaning, cause, benefit, pillar, condition, and ruling. The evidence is from Quran 4:92. Linguistically, diya is a verbal noun meaning the wealth given as a substitute for the slain. Legally, it means the compensation for life (as opposed to bodily injuries, which is arsh). The cause is error (khat'), but the ruling that a complete diya is obligatory for any free person—young or old, low or high, Muslim or dhimmi—is stated. Here the author introduces a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who holds that the diya of a dhimmi is only 4,000 dirhams (for Jews/Christians) and 800 for Magians. The Hanafi response, supported by a hadith and the practice of the caliphs, argues that dhimmis and Muslims are equal in inviolability and freedom, so their diya must be equal. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, so the full response is not given here.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): The financial compensation paid to the heirs of a person killed wrongfully; it is a substitute for the life.
خطأ (khata'): Mistake or accident, the cause of diya obligation as opposed to intentional killing.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under the protection of an Islamic state, paying jizya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Surah Al-Baqarah 2:178
B) Surah An-Nisa 4:92
C) Surah Al-Maidah 5:45
D) Surah Al-Isra 17:33

Key Takeaway
Diya is wajib for accidental killing of any free person of inviolable blood, including dhimmis, and the Hanafi school holds it equal to that of a Muslim based on prophetic practice and the reasoning of the early caliphs.

Composition of Diyat for Shibh al-Amd and Khata'
Specifies the composition of camels for semi-intentional and accidental killing, with an internal Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the composition of diyat for shibh al-amd and khata', and explain the internal dispute between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school divides the diya for shibh al-amd into four equal quarters of 25 camels each.

This lesson examines the specific composition of the 100-camel diya for semi-intentional killing (shibh al-amd) and accidental killing (khata'). The source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the main Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and contrasts it with the opinion of Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i. Understanding this dispute is crucial for accurately applying the rules of aggravated diya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of killing and their diya amounts
Shibh al-amd and khata' both have a diya of 100 camels, but the composition differs. Shibh al-amd is aggravated (mughallazah), while khata' is not.
Aggravation in shibh al-amd is due to the resemblance to intentional killing.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf)
For shibh al-amd, the 100 camels are divided into four equal quarters: 25 bint makhad, 25 bint labun, 25 hiqqa, and 25 jadha'a.
3
Identify the opposing position (Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i)
They hold a different composition: 30 hiqqa, 30 jadha'a, and 40 thaniyya (all pregnant).
This is based on a hadith specifying forty thaniyya.
4
Analyze the proofs for each side
The Hanafi proof relies on a hadith that the Prophet judged diya in quarters, implying shibh al-amd. The opposing side cites a specific hadith about shibh al-amd with distinct categories.
The Hanafi response interprets the opposing hadith differently and considers the quarters hadith more general.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference is the exact mix of camels required. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is relied upon for fatwa.
Ibn Mas'ud supported the Hanafi view, while Ali had a third view of thirds.

Source Text

The diya of shibh al-amd is one hundred camels in quarters, from bint makhad to jadha'a: meaning twenty-five bint makhad, twenty-five bint labun, and twenty-five jadha'a. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and al-Shafi'i said: thirty hiqqa, thirty jadha'a, and forty thaniyya with their young in their wombs, based on his saying (peace be upon him): 'Indeed, the slain of khata' al-'amd by whip, stick, and stone: in it is an aggravated diya of one hundred camels, forty of which are thaniyya to the bazil, all of them khalifa.' And because there is no dispute that aggravation is obligatory in it due to its resemblance to 'amd, and the meaning of aggravation is realized by imposing something that is not obligatory in khata'. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged regarding diya with one hundred camels in quarters, and it is known that he did not mean khata' because in khata' it is required in fifths, so it is known that the intended is shibh al-'amd. And because there is no dispute that diya is estimated at one hundred camels; he said (peace be upon him): 'In the life of a believer, one hundred camels.' And they differed regarding the manner of aggravation: Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) went that it is quarters like our madhhab; the madhhab of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) is that it is thirds: thirty-three hiqqa, thirty-three jadha'a, and thirty-four khalifa. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'And diya is not aggravated except in camels because the Shari'a came with it, and there is consensus on it. The fixed amounts cannot be known except by hearing, as there is no room for opinion in them, so it is not aggravated by other than them, even if a judge judges by it, his judgment is not executed due to lack of specification by estimation with other than camels.' He said: 'And in khata', one hundred camels in fifths, i.e., the diya of khata' is one hundred camels in fifths: bint makhad, etc.' That is.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage details the composition of diyat for shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional killing) and khata' (accidental killing). For shibh al-'amd, the diya is 100 camels divided into four categories: 25 bint makhad (one-year-old female camel), 25 bint labun (two-year-old female), 25 hiqqa (three-year-old female), and 25 jadha'a (four-year-old female). This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, however, hold a different composition: 30 hiqqa, 30 jadha'a, and 40 thaniyya (five-year-old female) carrying young, totaling 100 but in three categories. The evidence for the Hanafi position includes a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged diya for shibh al-'amd as 100 camels in quarters, and they argue that the phrase 'in quarters' distinguishes it from khata' (which is in fifths). An additional support is from the practice of Ibn Mas'ud. The passage also affirms that the aggravation (taghliz) of diya applies only to camels, not to gold or silver, because the Shari'a specifies only camels for taghliz, and this is established by consensus. For khata', the diya is 100 camels in fifths: 20 bint makhad, 20 bint labun, 20 hiqqa, 20 jadha'a, and 20 bint makhad (or similar). The text concludes by noting that the sharh author (presumably Ibn al-Humam or al-Babarti) explains the meaning.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Semi-intentional killing: where the killer intended to strike but not to kill, e.g., using a whip or stick.
دية مغلظة (diyah mughallazah): Aggravated diya, which for shibh al-'amd is 100 camels in a specific composition, reflecting the increased culpability.
بنت مخاض (bint makhad): A one-year-old female camel.
بنت لبون (bint labun): A two-year-old female camel.
حقة (hiqqa): A three-year-old female camel.
جذعة (jadha'a): A four-year-old female camel.
ثنية (thaniyya): A five-year-old female camel.
خلفة (khalifa): A pregnant female camel.
أخماس (akhmas): Fifth; the diya for khata' is divided into five equal categories.
أرباع (arba'): Quarters; the diya for shibh al-amd is divided into four equal categories.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The diya for shibh al-amd is 100 camels in four equal quarters according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Diyat for Shibh al-‘Amd: Composition, Dispute, and Scope of Taghliz
Definition, ruling, and intra-Hanafi dispute on diyat composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the diyah of shibh al-‘amd, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its camel composition, and identify the scope of taghliz.

Core Idea
Diyat shibh al-‘amd: 100 camels in four equal categories (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf) vs. 30+30+40 pregnant (Muhammad & al-Shafi‘i).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens the chapter on diyat by defining the term and clarifying its application to both whole-person compensation and limb injuries. It then proceeds to the ruling for quasi-deliberate homicide (shibh al-‘amd), specifying the obligatory camel composition. An intra-Hanafi dispute emerges between the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, with al-Shafi‘i siding with Muhammad. We will work through the definition, the ruling, the evidence, and the principle that taghliz (severity) applies only to camels.

Guided Path
1
Define diyah and its scope
Identify the term diyah as blood money for the whole soul, but note that it is also used for body parts in texts. The author reconciles this by citing al-‘Inaya's definition: a guarantee against a human or a part thereof.
This is a lexical clarification to avoid confusion when reading later chapters.
2
State the ruling for shibh al-‘amd
The diyah of shibh al-‘amd is wajib: 100 camels in fourths (arba‘an) from bint makhad to jadha‘a, i.e., 25 of each of the four categories according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The four categories are: bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, jadha‘a.
3
Contrast the opposing view
Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and al-Shafi‘i hold: 30 hiqqa, 30 jadha‘a, and 40 thaniyya (pregnant) in their wombs. This is based on a hadith that specifies 'arba‘oon minha thaniyah'. Identify the key difference in number and condition of pregnancy.
Pregnancy (khilfah) is an additional requirement for taghliz in their view.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence for each side
The majority rely on the Prophet's judgment that diyah is 'miah min al-ibil arba‘an', understood to refer to shibh al-‘amd because khata' uses fifths. Muhammad relies on the hadith about accidental-intentional killing with whip/stick/stone: 'arba‘oon minha thaniyah... kulluhun khalifah'. Compare the two proofs and note that the earlier one is considered more explicit.
Both are ahad hadith but the majority's is given preference in this school.
5
Apply the rule of taghliz
Taghliz (severity) applies only to camels, not to gold, silver, or other property. This is based on the Shari‘ah's specification and consensus; such apportionments are not subject to analogy. Even if a judge rules using other property, the judgment is void.
This underscores that muqaddarat (fixed amounts) are tawqifi (based on revelation).

Book of Diyat. Diyat of shibh al-‘amd is one hundred camels in fourths, from bint makhad to jadha‘a. And diyat is not made severe except in camels. And in khata' it is one hundred camels in fifths, or one thousand dinars or ten thousand dirhams. And their expiation... [the text continues]. The author says: The apparent meaning of all these mentioned is that diyah is specific to that which is the substitute for the soul, but this is contradicted by what will come in the next chapter: that in the nose there is diyah, in the tongue diyah, in the penis diyah, in the beard diyah, in the hair of the head diyah, in the eyebrows diyah, in the eyes diyah, in the hands diyah, in the feet diyah, etc., in which diyah is applied to substitutes for less than the soul. Likewise, what is reported in the hadith: Sa‘id ibn al-Musayyab narrated that the Prophet said: 'In the soul and in the tongue there is diyah, and in the nose...' and this is the document that the Prophet wrote to ‘Amr ibn Hazm. So the most apparent interpretation of diyah is what the author of al-‘Inaya mentioned finally: 'Diyah is a name for a guarantee that is obligated in exchange for a human or a part of him, usually paid because pardon rarely occurs due to the great sanctity of the human.' Since the purpose of fiqh is to explain rulings, not realities, the author left the explanation of the reality and began to explain its types. He said: (The diyah of shibh al-‘amd is one hundred camels in fourths from bint makhad to jadha‘a), meaning twenty-five bint makhad, twenty-five bint labun, and twenty-five jadha‘a. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and al-Shafi‘i said: thirty hiqqa, thirty jadha‘a, and forty thaniyya pregnant with their offspring, due to his saying: 'Beware, the killed of accidental-intentional by whip, stick, and stone carries a severe diyah of one hundred camels, forty of which are thaniyya to bazil, all of them pregnant.' And because there is no dispute that taghliz is obligatory due to its resemblance to ‘amd, and the meaning of taghliz is achieved by requiring something not required in khata'. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that the Prophet judged diyah as one hundred camels in fourths, and it is known that he did not mean khata' because it requires fifths, so it is understood that he meant shibh al-‘amd. And because there is no dispute that diyah is apportioned as one hundred camels; he said: 'In the soul of a believer one hundred camels.' And they differed over the description of taghliz: Ibn Mas‘ud went to fourths like our madhhab, and ‘Ali's madhhab was thirds: thirty-three hiqqa, thirty-three jadha‘a, and thirty-four khalifa. He said: (And diyah is not made severe except in camels) because the Shari‘ah has come with it and there is consensus on it, and apportioned things are only known through hearing, as there is no room for opinion in them, so it is not made severe with other than camels; even if a judge judges with it, his judgment is not executed due to lack of authoritative determination of the apportionment with other than camels.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the chapter of diyat. It begins by defining diyah and clarifying a potential contradiction: while diyah is usually the blood money for the whole soul, it is also used for body parts. The author resolves this by citing al-'Inaya's definition that diyah is a guarantee against a human or a part thereof. Then the ruling on shibh al-‘amd is given: 100 camels in four categories (bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, jadha'a) according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, while Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and al-Shafi‘i hold a different composition (30 hiqqa, 30 jadha‘a, 40 pregnant thaniyya). The dispute is settled by adducing hadith proof: the Prophet's judgment in a case of shibh al-‘amd specifying the four categories, and the hadith on accidental-intentional killing requiring a severe diyah. The passage then notes that taghliz (severity in diyat) applies only to camels, not to gold or silver, based on the Shari'ah's specification and consensus.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money payable as compensation for killing or injuring a person; specifically defined as a substitute for the soul (nafs) but also used for bodily parts.
شبه العمد (shibh al-‘amd): Qasl (killing) that resembles intentional but is not fully intentional, e.g., striking with a stick without intent to kill; its diyah is severe (mughallazah).
تغليظ (taghliz): Severity in diyah, meaning the composition of camels is more stringent than in accidental killing (e.g., pregnant animals, specific ages).
أرباع (arba‘an): Four equal parts, i.e., 25 camels from each of four age categories.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied upon) composition of the diyah for shibh al-‘amd in the Hanafi school?
A) 30 hiqqa, 30 jadha‘a, 40 pregnant thaniyya
B) 25 of each: bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, jadha‘a
C) 33.3 hiqqa, 33.3 jadha‘a, 33.4 khalifa
D) 20 of each of five categories

Key Takeaway
The diyah of shibh al-‘amd is 100 camels in four equal categories (mufta bihi), and taghliz (severity) applies only to camels, not to other forms of payment.

Diyat for Accidental Killing: Camel Composition and Gold/Silver Option
Ruling on diyat for khata' with proofs and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyat for accidental killing (khata') regarding camel composition and monetary option, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-arguments.

Core Idea
Diyat for khata' is 100 camels in five equal categories or 1,000 gold dinars = 10,000 silver dirhams.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the Diyat for accidental homicide (khata'), specifying both the camel composition and the alternative gold/silver amount. It also addresses two key disputes: al-Shafi'i's replacement of one camel type and the amount of dirhams (10,000 vs. 12,000). Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi position and its rational basis through hadith and financial analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (accidental killing/khata') and the general obligation (wajib) of diyat. Note the two options: camels or gold/silver.
The diyat is lighter than for intentional killing—no taghliz (aggravation).
2
Analyze the camel composition
Examine the five categories: 20 each of ibn makhad, bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, jadha‘a. Understand the hadith evidence from Ibn Mas‘ud.
Al-Shafi'i substitutes ibn labun for ibn makhad; Hanafi rebuts using the same hadith and reasoning of alleviation.
3
Examine the monetary option
Note that 1,000 dinars or 10,000 dirhams is an alternative. Understand the Shafi'i/Maliki claim of 12,000 dirhams and their hadith from Ibn ‘Abbas.
4
Follow the Hanafi response to the dirham dispute
Trace the argument: difference due to dirham weight (weight-five vs. weight-six). The Hanafi figure uses the weight-six dirham, which is more reliable according to historical analysis by al-Khabbazi.
Additional supporting evidence from ghurrah (fetus diyat) and zakat nisab reinforces 10,000 dirhams.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under Hanafi, diyat is 10,000 dirhams; under Shafi'i/Maliki, it is 12,000 dirhams. Also, the camel category difference affects which types are delivered.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Hanafi position as argued in this text.

النبي اديته اثني عشر ألفاً. رواه أبو داود والترمذي. ولأنه لا خلاف أنها من الدنانير ألف دينار وكانت قيمة الدينار على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اثني عشر درهماً. ولنا ما روي عن ابن عمر رضي الله قيراطاً ضرورة استوائهما، ووزن الستة يكون نصف الدينار وعشرة فيكون اثني عشر قيراطاً وزن الخمسة يكون نصف الدينار فيكون عشرة قراريط فيكون المجموع اثنين واربعين قيراطاً، فإن جعلتها أثلاثاً صار كل ثلث أربعة عشر قيراطاً الذي كان عليه دراهمهم. فإذا حمل ما رواه الشافعي على وزن خمسة وما رويناه على وزن ستة استويا. والذي يرجح مذهبنا ما روي أن الواجب في الجنين خمسمائة درهم وهو عشر دية الأم عنده سواء كان ذكراً وهو
He said: (And in khata' one hundred camels in fifths), meaning the diyah of khata' is one hundred camels in fifths: ibn makhad etc., i.e., twenty ibn makhad, twenty bint makhad, twenty bint labun, twenty hiqqa, twenty jadha‘a. So when it is in fifths, from each of these types there are twenty, based on what Ibn Mas‘ud narrated that the Prophet said in the diyah of khata': 'Twenty hiqqa, twenty jadha‘a, twenty bint makhad, twenty bint labun, twenty bint makhad.' It was narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, Ahmad and others. Al-Shafi‘i adopted our madhhab except that he said twenty ibn labun is required instead of ibn makhad. The proof against him is what we narrated, and because what we say is lighter as ibn makhad is placed in the position of ibn labun, so that labun is more fitting for the condition of the mistaken one; and because the Shari'ah made ibn labun equivalent to bint makhad in zakat, taking it in its place, so requiring twenty of it along with twenty bint makhad is like requiring forty bint makhad, which is not fitting, rather not permissible due to lack of differentiation; and because the Prophet intended only to lighten by changing the ages of camels, and lightening is not achieved in this, so it is not permissible. He said: (Or one thousand dinars or ten thousand dirhams). Malik and al-Shafi‘i said: the diyah is twelve thousand dirhams, based on Ibn ‘Abbas that a man was killed and the Prophet made his diyah twelve thousand. It was narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. And because there is no dispute that it is one thousand dinars, and the value of a dinar in the time of the Prophet was twelve dirhams. Our proof is what is narrated from Ibn ‘Umar that the Prophet judged diyah for a killed person as ten thousand dirhams. And what we said is preferable for certainty because it is less. Or it may be interpreted that what they narrated was based on a weight of five (dirhams to a dinar) and what we narrated was based on a weight of six. And thus their dirhams were from the time of the Prophet until the time of ‘Umar, according to what al-Khabbazi narrated in the book of zakat: he said: 'The dirhams in the time of the Prophet were three types: one had a weight of ten, meaning ten of them weigh ten dinars—that is the value of a dinar; the second had a weight of six, meaning ten of them weigh six...' until the end. So ‘Umar combined the three and mixed them, making them three dirhams, so a third of the total became a dirham. This reveals that the dinar is twenty qirats, over ten it is like twenty qirats necessarily of their equality; and the weight six is half a dinar and ten so it becomes twelve qirats; the weight five is half a dinar so it becomes ten qirats; the total is forty-two qirats. If you make them thirds, each third becomes fourteen qirats, which was the basis of their dirhams. So if what al-Shafi‘i narrated is taken as weight five, and what we narrated as weight six, they become equal. And what supports our madhhab is that it is narrated that the wajib for a fetus is five hundred dirhams, which is one-tenth of the mother's diyah according to him, whether male or female; but according to us it is one-tenth of the diyah of the soul if female, and half of one-tenth if male. So it is known from this that the mother's diyah is five thousand and the man's is double that, which is ten thousand. And because we have consensus that it is from gold: one thousand dinars, and the dinar is valued in Shari'ah at ten dirhams. Do you not see that the nisab of silver in zakat is apportioned as two hundred dirhams, and the nisab of gold therein as twenty dinars, so one is wealthy with this amount of each, as zakat is only obligatory on the wealthy. So it is known by necessary knowledge that the dinar is apportioned...

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on diyat for accidental killing (khata'). It first details the five categories of camels: 20 each of ibn makhad, bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqa, jadha‘a. The hadith of Ibn Mas‘ud is cited as evidence. Then a dispute with al-Shafi‘i is addressed: al-Shafi‘i would replace ibn makhad with ibn labun. The Hanafi rebuttal argues that this would lead to duplication and defeat the purpose of alleviation. Next, the option of paying in gold or silver is discussed: the Hanafi position is 1,000 dinars or 10,000 dirhams, while Malik and al-Shafi‘i say 12,000 dirhams. A detailed financial analysis follows, comparing the weights of dirhams in the prophetic era, citing al-Khabbazi, to reconcile the difference: the 12,000 dirhams reported by al-Shafi‘i are based on a lighter dirham (weight of five qirats per dinar), while the Hanafi 10,000 are based on weight six. The passage concludes with an argument from the diyah of a fetus (ghurrah) to support the Hanafi figure, showing internal consistency.

Key Terms
الخطأ (al-khata'): Accidental killing, where there is no intent to kill nor act directly intended; its diyah is lighter (not severe) and paid in five categories or gold/silver.
أخماس (akhmas): Five parts, i.e., the diyah of khata' is divided into five equal categories of camels (20 each).
دينار (dinar): Gold coin; in the Hanafi view, its value is 10 dirhams, leading to diyah of 1,000 dinars = 10,000 dirhams.
درهم (dirham): Silver coin; its weight varied; the Hanafi diyah uses the weight-six dirham, resulting in 10,000 dirhams.
ابن مخاض (ibn makhad): A male camel entering its second year (by some definitions, or a one-year-old). One of the five categories.
جنين (janin): Fetus; its diyah (ghurrah) is 500 dirhams, used as evidence for the full diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 12,000 dirhams
B) 10,000 dirhams
C) 8,000 dirhams
D) 5,000 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi diyat for accidental killing is 100 camels in five categories or 1,000 gold dinars / 10,000 silver dirhams, supported by hadith and historical weight analysis.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

Types of Diyat and the Killer's Choice: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Garments
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyat types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the types of property valid for diyat and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, identifying the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The killer may choose among camels, cows, and sheep for diyat; garments are not a valid type according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the types of property that can be used to pay diyat (blood-money). While the obligation of diyat itself is agreed upon, the specific items accepted vary between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this khilaf and the reasoning behind the preferred view is essential for correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute is about which types of property are valid for paying diyat. Abu Hanifa restricts to three types; the Sahibayn include a fourth.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that only camels, cows, and sheep are valid. His dalil is qiyas: diyat must be of known value, and camels are established by famous traditions.
He argues that other items like garments have unknown value, so they cannot be used.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their evidence
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) add garments (حلل) as a fourth type. Their evidence includes the hadith of Jabir and the practice of Caliph Umar.
The hadith explicitly lists garments among the prescribed types.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing evidence
The Hanafi response argues that the hadith is not binding because it may have been a judgment by conciliation (sulh), not a legal prescription. Garments are of unknown value, and the established qiyas with camels cannot be overridden by a weak tradition.
The author of al-Hidayah supports Abu Hanifa's reliance on qiyas.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view. This is based on the need for known value, the weakness of the hadith as a binding proof, and the strength of qiyas.
The killer's choice is limited to three types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر في النص ولا يجوز الإطعام والجنين ويجوز الرضيع لو أحد أبويه مسلماً ودية المرأة على النصف من دية الرجل في النفس وفيما دونها ودية المسلم والذمي سواء. [بعشرة دراهم؟]. ثم الخيار في هذه الأنواع الثلاثة إلى القاتل لأنه هو الذي يجب عليه فيكون الخيار إليه كما في كفارة اليمين ولا تثبت الدية إلا إن هذه الأنواع الثلاثة عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وقالا: يجب [منها] ومن البقر مائتا بقرة ومن الغنم ألفا شاة ومن الحلل مائتا حلة كل حلة ثوبان لما روي عن جابر رضي الله عنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فرض في الدية على أهل الإبل مائة من الإبل، وعلى أهل البقر مائتي بقرة وعلى أهل [؟] الشياه ألفي شاة وعلى أهل الحلل مائتي حلة. رواه أبو داود. وكان عمر رضي الله عنه يقضي بذلك على أهل كل مال كما ذكرنا. وكل حلة ثوبان إزار ورداء وهو المختار. وفي النهاية: قيل في زماننا قميص وسراويل وله أن التقدير إنما يستقيم بشيء معلوم المالية وهذه الأشياء مجهولة المالية ولهذا لا يقدر بها ضمان المتلفات، والتقدير بالإبل عرف بالآثار المشهورة ولم يوجد ذلك في غيرها فلا يعدل عن القياس، والآثار التي وردت فيها تحتمل القضاء فيها بطريق الصلح فلا يلزم حجة. وذكر في المعاقل أنه لو صالح على الزيادة على مائتي حلة أن مائتي بقرة لا يجوز وتأويله أنه قولهما.
It is mentioned in the text: 'And feeding and the fetus are not permissible; the suckling infant is permissible if one of his parents is Muslim; the blood-money of a woman is half the blood-money of a man for the life and for what is less than it; the blood-money of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal. [With ten dirhams?]. Then the choice among these three types is given to the killer, because it is he upon whom it is obligatory, so the choice is given to him as in the expiation of an oath. The blood-money is not established except that these three types [are valid] according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. The two said (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): 'From them, and from cows two hundred cows, and from sheep two thousand sheep, and from garments two hundred garments, each garment being two cloths', based on what was narrated from Jabir, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Prophet, peace be upon him, prescribed for blood-money among the people of camels one hundred camels, and among the people of cows two hundred cows, and among the people of these sheep two thousand sheep, and among the people of garments two hundred garments. Abu Dawud narrated it. Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, used to judge accordingly among the people of each wealth as we mentioned. Each garment is two cloths: a waist-wrapper and a cloak, and this is the chosen [view]. In al-Nihaya: it was said in our time: a shirt and trousers. And it is his [i.e., the author's] view that the estimation is only valid with something of known value, and these things are of unknown value, therefore compensation for damaged goods is not estimated by them. Estimation by camels is known through famous traditions, and that is not found for others, so deviation from analogy is not allowed. The traditions that came concerning them are open to [the interpretation] that the judgment was by way of conciliation, so they are not binding. It is mentioned in al-Ma'aqil that if he settled for an increase of two hundred garments over two hundred cows, it is not permissible, and its interpretation is that it is according to the two's [i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's] view.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage opens an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the types of property acceptable for payment of diyat (blood-money) and the killer's right to choose among them. The matn initially lists several rulings, but the focus here is on the types of diyat. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, only three types of property are valid for diyat: camels, cows, and sheep. The killer has the choice to pay any of these three. This is based on the principle that the obligation lies with the killer, so he chooses, similar to the expiation of an oath. The two companions (Sahibayn), Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, hold that garments (حلل) are also a valid type, making four types in total. They support their position with a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah that the Prophet prescribed camels for camel owners, cows for cow owners, sheep for sheep owners, and garments for garment owners. They also cite the practice of Caliph Umar who judged accordingly. The author of al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani) states that the preferred view is that a garment consists of two cloths: an izār (waist-wrapper) and a ridā' (cloak), though in later times it may be a shirt and trousers. The commentary then provides a rationale: estimation of diyat must be by something of known monetary value, and camels are established by famous traditions, while other items are of unknown value. The traditions regarding cows, sheep, and garments are open to interpretation as being by way of conciliation (sulh) and not binding. Therefore, Abu Hanifa's restriction to camels alone (or to the three types) is based on qiyas (analogy) and rejection of weak traditions. The passage ends with a note from al-Ma'aqil that settlement for excess over two hundred garments or two hundred cows is invalid, which aligns with the view of the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood-money or financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim in cases of homicide or bodily injury.
خيار (khiyar): Choice given to the killer among the valid types of diyat payment.
حلة (hullah): A garment set consisting of two cloths; in this context, a type of property accepted for diyat by Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how many types of property are valid for payment of diyat?
A) Four
B) Three
C) Two
D) One

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position limits diyat to camels, cows, and sheep; garments are not a valid type.
