Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Definitions of Head Wounds: Dami'a, Badi'a, Mutalahima
Commentary on definitions of early head wound types with intra-Hanafi difference on order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the first five types of head wounds (kharisa, dami'a, damiya, badi'a, mutalahima) and state the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the order of badi'a and mutalahima.

Core Idea
Head wound definitions distinguish depth; linguistic differences do not alter legal rulings.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq dives into the precise definitions of the early types of head wounds (shijaj). Understanding these terms is essential because they determine which fixed amounts (muqaddarat) apply. The commentary also resolves a minor intra-Hanafi disagreement on the order of badi'a and mutalahima, showing that the dispute is lexical, not legal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound types mentioned
List the first five types: kharisa, dami'a, damiya, badi'a, mutalahima. Note their linguistic roots.
Kharisa splits skin; dami'a oozes tear-like blood; damiya bleeds without flow; badi'a cuts flesh; mutalahima cuts and removes flesh.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Contrast Imam Muhammad's view (mutalahima before badi'a) with Abu Yusuf and Zahir al-Riwayah (badi'a before mutalahima). Understand the basis: Muhammad focuses on 'joining' (iltiham), while the majority focuses on depth of cut.
The Zahir al-Riwayah position is the mufta bihi.
3
Note the legal principle
The passage states that the difference is in derivation (makhadh al-ishtiqaq), not in hukm. Thus, regardless of order, the fixed diya amounts for these wounds are unaffected.

Source Text

كتاب الديات
هي : العين، سميت بها لأن الدم يخرج منها بقدر الدمع من القلة وقيل لأن عينيه تدمع بسبب ألم يحصل له منها. وفي المحيط الدامعة التي يخرج منها ما يشبه الدمع مأخوذة من دمع والدامية وهي التي يسيل منها الدم. وذكر المرغيناني أن الدامية هي التي تدمي من غير أن يسيل منها دم هو الصحيح يروى عن أبي عبيد والدامعة وهي التي يسيل منها الدم كدمع العين. ومن قال أن صاحبها تدمع عيناه من الألم فقد أبعد والباضعة وهي التي تبضع الجلد أي تقطعه مأخوذة من البضع وهو الشق والقطع ومنه مبضع الفصاد. أقول: في تفسير الباضعة بما ذكره الشارح فتور وإن تابعه صاحب الكافي وكثير من المتأخرين فيه لأن قطع الجلد متحقق في الصورة الأولى منها لا سيما في الدامعة والدامية إذا الظاهر أن شيئاً من إظهار الدم وأصالته لا يتصور بدون قطع الجلد وقد صرح الشراح بتحقق قطع الجلد في كل الأنواع العشرة للشجة فكان التفسير المذكور شاملاً للكل غير مختص بالباضعة، فالظاهر في تفسير الباضعة هو ما ذكر في المحيط والبدائع حيث قال في المحيط ثم الباضعة وهي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه. وقال في البدائع والباضعة هي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه ا هـ. ويعضد ذلك ما وقع في معتبرات كتب اللغة قال في المغرب وفي الشجاج الباضعة وهي التي جرحت الجلد وشقت اللحم ا هـ . وقال في الصحاح : الباضعة الشجة التي تقطع الجلد وتشق اللحم وتدمي إلا أنها لا تسيل الدم. وقال في القاموس والباضعة الشجة التي تقطع الجلد وتشق اللحم شقاً خفيفاً وتدمي إلا أنها لا تسيل الدم ا هـ. لا يقال فعلى هذا يلزم تشبيه الباضعة بالمتلاحمة فإنهم قالوا والمتلاحمة هي التي تأخذ في اللحم وهذا في المآل غير ما نقلته عن المحيط والبدائع في تفسير الباضعة لأنا نقول : من فسر الباضعة بما لا يقول بتفسير المتلاحمة بما ذكر حتى يلزم الاشتباه بل يزيد عليه قيداً، وعن هذا قال في المحيط : ثم الباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه قال شيخ الإسلام : ولا تنزع شيئاً من اللحم . ثم المتلاحمة وهي التي تقطع اللحم وتنزع شيئاً من اللحم؛ إلى هنا لفظ المحيط . وقال في البدائع : والباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه، والمتلاحمة التي تذهب في اللحم أكثر مما تذهب الباضعة فيه. وقال في المغرب والمتلاحمة من الشجاج هي التي تشق اللحم دون العظم ثم تتلاحم بعد شقها أي تتلاءم ا.هـ. وقال في الصحاح : والمتلاحمة الشجة التي أخذت في اللحم دون العظم ثم تتلاحم ولم تبلغ السمحاق ا هـ . وقال في القاموس : وشجة متلاحمة أخذت فيه ولم تبلغ السمحاق والمتلاحمة وهي التي تأخذ في اللحم كله ثم تتلاحم بعد ذلك أي تلتئم وتتلاصق سميت بذلك تفاؤلاً على ما يؤول إليه. وروي عن محمد أن المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة لأن المتلاحمة من قولهم التحم الشيئان إذا اتصل أحدهما بالآخر التي تظهر اللحم ولا تقطعه والباضعة بعدها لأنها تقطعه. وفي ظاهر الرواية والمتلاحمة تعمل في قطع أكثر اللحم وهي بعد الباضعة . وقال الأزهري : الأوجه أن يقال فالمتلاحمة هي قلنا من المعنى الظاهر هي
المتلاحمة أي القاطعة للحم والاختلاف الذي وجد في الشجاج راجع إلى مأخذ الاشتقاق لا إلى الحكم والسمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى السمحاق وهي الجلدة الرقيقة التي بين اللحم وعظم الرأس، والموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم أي تبينه، والهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، والمنقلة وهي التي تنقل العظم بعد الكسر أي تحوله والآمة وهي التي تصل إلى أم الدماغ، وأم الدماغ هي الجلدة الرقيقة التي تجمع الدماغ، وبعد الآمة شجة تسمى الدامغة بالغبن المعجمة وهي التي تصل إلى الدماغ لم يذكرها محمد لأن النفس لا تبقى بعدها عادة فتكون قتلاً، ولا تكون من الشجاج والكلام في الشجاج ولذا لم يذكر الخارصة والدامغة لأنها لا يبقى لها في الغالب أثر . وهذه الشجاج تختص بالرأس والوجه وما كان في غيرهما يسمى جراحة فهذا هو الحقيقة والحكم يترتب على الحقيقة فلا يجب بالجراحة ما يجب بالشجة من المقدار لأن التقدير بالنقل وهو إنما ورد في الشجاع وهي تختص بالرأس والوجه فخص الحكم المقدم بها، ولا يجوز إلحاق الجراحة بها دلالة ولا قياساً لأنها ليست في معناها في الشين لأن الوجه والرأس يظهران في الغالب وغيرهما مستور غالباً لا يظهر. واختلفوا في اللحيين فعندهما في الوجه فيتحقق الشجاج فيهما فيجب فيها موجبها خلافاً لما يقول مالك رحمه الله فإنه يقول إنهما ليسا من الوجه لأن المواجهة لا تقع بهما، ونحن نقول هما متصلان بالوجه من غير فاصل ويتحقق معنى المواجهة فصار كالذقن لأنهما تحتها. وقال شيخ الإسلام ويجب أن يفرض غسلهما في الوضوء لأنهما من الوجه حقيقة إلا أنا تركناهما للإجماع ولا إجماع هنا فبقينا العيرة للحقيقة .
وفي المبسوط : الشجاج في الرأس والوجه أحد عشر أولها الخارصة وهي تشق الجلد مأخوذة من قولهم خرص القصار الثوب إذا شقه من الدق ثم الدامعة وهي التي يخرج منها ما يشبه الدمع مأخوذة من دمع العين. ولم يذكرها محمد لأنها لم يبق لها أثر في الغالب، ثم التي يخرج منها الدم ثم الباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم، ثم المتلاحمة، وعن محمد أنه جعل المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة خلافاً لأبي يوسف وتفسيرها عند أبي يوسف التي تقشر الجلد وتجمع اللحم في موضع الجراحة ولا تقطعه مأخوذة من التحام؛ يقال التحم الجيشان إذا اجتمعا . ثم السمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى جلدة رقيقة فوق العظم تسمى السمحاق، ثم الموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم واللحم، ثم الهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، ثم المنقلة التي يخرج منها العظم لأنها تكسر العظم وتنقله عن موضعه، ثم الآمة التي تصل إلى أم الرأس وهي الجلدة التي فوق الدماغ، ثم الدامغة التي تخرق الجلد وتصل إلى الدماغ ولم يذكرها محمد لأن الإنسان لا يعيش معها. وأما أحكامها فإن كانت هذه الشجاج . ففي الموضحة القصاص لأن السكين ينتهي إلى العظم ولا يخاف منه الهلاك غالباً فيجب القصاص لقوله تعالى والجروح قصاص [المائدة : ٥] وذكر الكرخي عنه أنه ليس في شيء من عمداً
Book of Blood Money. It is: the eye, named so because blood flows from it like tears due to sparseness, or because one's eyes tear due to pain. In al-Muhit: ad-dami‘a is that from which something resembling tears flows, taken from tears; ad-damiya is that from which blood flows. Al-Marghinani mentioned that ad-damiya is that which bleeds without blood flowing—that is correct, related from Abu ‘Ubayd; ad-dami‘a is that from which blood flows like tears. Whoever says that the person's eyes tear from pain has gone far. Al-badi‘a is that which splits the skin, i.e., cuts it, taken from al-bad‘ meaning splitting and cutting, from which is the lancet. I say: the interpretation of al-badi‘a as mentioned by the commentator is weak, even if followed by the author of al-Kafi and many later scholars, because cutting the skin is realized in the first form, especially in ad-dami‘a and ad-damiya, since it is apparent that nothing of blood manifestation and essence is conceivable without cutting the skin. The commentators have explicitly stated the realization of cutting the skin in all ten types of head wound. So the mentioned interpretation covers all, not specific to al-badi‘a. The apparent interpretation of al-badi‘a is what is mentioned in al-Muhit and al-Bada’i‘: in al-Muhit, 'then al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it.' In al-Bada’i‘: 'al-badi‘a is that which cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it.' This is supported by reliable lexicons: in al-Mughrib: 'among head wounds, al-badi‘a is that which wounds the skin and splits the flesh.' In as-Sihah: 'al-badi‘a is the head wound that cuts the skin and splits the flesh and bleeds but does not cause blood to flow.' In al-Qamus: 'al-badi‘a is the head wound that cuts the skin and splits the flesh slightly and bleeds but does not cause blood to flow.' It cannot be said that this causes al-badi‘a to resemble al-mutalahima, because they said al-mutalahima is that which penetrates the flesh. This is ultimately not what I transmitted from al-Muhit and al-Bada’i‘ regarding the interpretation of al-badi‘a, for we say: the one who interprets al-badi‘a as such does not interpret al-mutalahima as mentioned such that confusion arises; rather, he adds a condition. For this, al-Muhit said: 'then al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it. Shaykh al-Islam said: it does not remove any flesh. Then al-mutalahima: it cuts the flesh and removes some flesh.' Up to here is the wording of al-Muhit. Al-Bada’i‘ said: 'al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it. Al-mutalahima: it goes into the flesh more than al-badi‘a does.' Al-Mughrib said: 'al-mutalahima among head wounds is that which splits the flesh without [reaching] the bone, then heals after splitting, i.e., becomes coherent.' As-Sihah said: 'al-mutalahima is the head wound that penetrates the flesh without the bone, then heals and does not reach as-simhaq.' Al-Qamus said: 'a mutalahima head wound: it penetrated the flesh and did not reach as-simhaq. Al-mutalahima is that which penetrates all the flesh then heals afterwards, i.e., closes and adheres; named so optimistically for its outcome.' It is related from Muhammad that al-mutalahima comes before al-badi‘a because al-mutalahima is from their saying 'the two things joined' when one connects with the other, which shows the flesh and does not cut it; al-badi‘a comes after because it cuts it. In the apparent narration (Zahir ar-Riwaya), al-mutalahima works in cutting more flesh and comes after al-badi‘a. Al-Azhari said: the most appropriate is to say al-mutalahima is, we say from the apparent meaning, it is the cutter of flesh. The difference found in head wounds refers to the derivation, not to the ruling. As-simhaq is that which reaches as-simhaq, which is the thin membrane between the flesh and the skull. Al-mudiha is that which exposes the bone, i.e., makes it apparent. Al-hashima is that which crushes the bone. Al-munaqqila is that which moves the bone after breakage, i.e., displaces it. Al-ammah is that which reaches the mother of the brain, and the mother of the brain is the thin membrane that contains the brain. After al-ammah there is a wound called ad-damigha (with a dotted ghayn) which reaches the brain; Muhammad did not mention it because life usually does not remain after it, so it is killing, and it is not among head wounds; the discussion is about head wounds. For this reason, he did not mention al-kharisa and ad-damigha because they usually leave no mark. These head wounds are specific to the head and face; what is elsewhere is called a wound (jarahah). This is the reality, and the ruling is based on reality. So what is obligatory for a head wound is not obligatory for a wound in terms of amount, because the determination is by transmitted texts, and that was only reported for head wounds, which are specific to the head and face, so the ruling is specific to them. It is not permissible to analogize wounds to them by implication or qiyas, because they are not similar in shamefulness, since the face and head are usually apparent, while others are usually covered, not apparent. They differed regarding the two jaws (al-lahyayn). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they are part of the face, so head wounds are realized on them, and their due is obligatory. Contrary to what Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) says, he says they are not part of the face because facing does not occur with them. We say: they are connected to the face without a separator and the meaning of facing is realized, so they become like the chin because they are below it. Shaykh al-Islam said: it would be obligatory to prescribe washing them in wudu' because they are actually part of the face, except we omitted it due to consensus; but here there is no consensus, so we adhere to the reality. In al-Mabsut: head wounds on the head and face are eleven. First: al-kharisa, which splits the skin, taken from their saying 'the fuller split the cloth when beating it.' Then ad-dami‘a, from which something resembling tears flows, taken from tears of the eye; Muhammad did not mention it because it usually leaves no effect. Then that from which blood flows. Then al-badi‘a, which cuts the flesh. Then al-mutalahima. From Muhammad that he placed al-mutalahima before al-badi‘a, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Its interpretation according to Abu Yusuf is that it peels the skin and collects the flesh at the site of the wound without cutting it, taken from iltiham; it is said 'the two armies joined' when they gathered. Then as-simhaq, which reaches a thin membrane above the bone called as-simhaq. Then al-mudiha, which exposes the bone and flesh. Then al-hashima, which crushes the bone. Then al-munaqqila, from which bone comes out, because it breaks the bone and moves it from its place. Then al-ammah, which reaches the mother of the head, which is the membrane above the brain. Then ad-damigha, which pierces the skin and reaches the brain; Muhammad did not mention it because a person does not live with it. As for their rulings: if these head wounds occur intentionally, then for al-mudiha there is qisas because the knife reaches the bone and death is not usually feared, so qisas is obligatory due to His statement: 'And wounds are subject to retaliation' (al-Ma'idah 5:45). Al-Karkhi mentioned from him that there is not in anything of intentional...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the types of head wounds (shijaj) and their rulings. The author first defines various terms for wounds on the head and face, giving linguistic derivations and quoting earlier authorities like al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', and lexicons. He distinguishes between dami'a (blood flows like tears) and damiya (blood appears but does not flow), and explains the difference between badi'a (cuts flesh without removing any) and mutalahima (cuts and removes some flesh). He notes a difference of opinion between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the order of these two, with Imam Muhammad placing mutalahima before badi'a, but the Zahir al-Riwayah placing it after. The commentary clarifies that these differences are linguistic and do not affect the legal ruling. Next, the passage defines simhaq (reaches the membrane above bone), mudiha (exposes bone), hashima (crushes bone), munaqqila (displaces bone), ammah (reaches the brain membrane), and damigha (penetrates to brain, usually fatal). It emphasizes that head wounds (shijaj) are specific to the head and face; wounds elsewhere are called jarahah and are not subject to the fixed amounts set for shijaj. The author then discusses the inclusion of the jaws (lahyayn): according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, they are part of the face and thus shijaj apply, contrary to Malik. Finally, the passage states the ruling on qisas for mudiha: retaliation is obligatory because the knife reaches the bone and death is not typically feared, citing the Quranic verse 'and wounds are subject to retaliation'. The mention of al-Karkhi suggests a transmission that qisas is not applicable in some intentional cases, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
الشجة (ash-shajjah): A wound specific to the head or face, classified by depth and severity into types such as dami'a, badi'a, mutalahima, etc.
الدامعة (ad-dami'a): A head wound from which blood flows like tears, seeping slowly.
الباضعة (al-badi'a): A head wound that cuts the flesh (not just skin) but does not remove any flesh.
المتلاحمة (al-mutalahima): A head wound that cuts the flesh and removes some flesh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah (the relied-upon narration), which head wound type comes after badi'a?
A) Dami'a
B) Mutalahima
C) Kharisa
D) Simhaq

Key Takeaway
The order of badi'a and mutalahima differs among Hanafi imams, but the mufta bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah order (badi'a first), and the difference is linguistic, not legal.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Higher Head Wounds and the Scope of Shijaj
Definitions of deep head wounds, specificity to head/face, and the jaw dispute with Malik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the remaining head wound types (simhaq, mudiha, hashima, munaqqila, ammah, damigha) and explain why shijaj are restricted to the head/face, including the Hanafi-Maliki dispute on the jaws.

Core Idea
Head wounds are limited to the head and face; jaws are part of the face per Hanafis, contra Malik.

This part of the commentary continues the classification with deeper wounds that reach the bone or brain. The author emphasizes that shijaj are exclusively head/face wounds, a key ruling that prevents analogizing wounds elsewhere. The dispute with Imam Malik over the jaws (lahyayn) serves as a test case for the principle that reality ('haqiqah') governs unless displaced by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six higher types
List: simhaq (reaches membrane over bone), mudiha (exposes bone), hashima (crushes bone), munaqqila (displaces bone), ammah (reaches brain membrane), damigha (penetrates brain). Note that damigha is usually fatal and excluded from shijaj.
Muhammad ibn al-Hasan did not mention damigha because life does not continue after it.
2
Understand the head/face specificity
The passage states that shijaj are specific to the head and face; elsewhere they are called jarahah. The fixed amounts (muqaddarat) for shijaj do not apply to jarahah. The reason is that head/face are usually visible, unlike other body parts.
No qiyas is allowed to extend shijaj rules to other parts because the shame factor (shayn) is different.
3
Analyze the jaw dispute
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad include the jaws (lahyayn) in the face, so shijaj rulings apply. Malik excludes them, treating jaw wounds as ordinary jarahah. The Hanafi evidence: jaws are connected without a separator and facing (muwajaha) is realized. A counter-argument from wudu is dismissed due to consensus there, but no consensus here.
The dispute has practical effect: for Malik, wounds on the jaws have no fixed diya amounts; for Hanafis, they do.

هي : العين، سميت بها لأن الدم يخرج منها بقدر الدمع من القلة وقيل لأن عينيه تدمع بسبب ألم يحصل له منها. وفي المحيط الدامعة التي يخرج منها ما يشبه الدمع مأخوذة من دمع والدامية وهي التي يسيل منها الدم. وذكر المرغيناني أن الدامية هي التي تدمي من غير أن يسيل منها دم هو الصحيح يروى عن أبي عبيد والدامعة وهي التي يسيل منها الدم كدمع العين. ومن قال أن صاحبها تدمع عيناه من الألم فقد أبعد والباضعة وهي التي تبضع الجلد أي تقطعه مأخوذة من البضع وهو الشق والقطع ومنه مبضع الفصاد. أقول: في تفسير الباضعة بما ذكره الشارح فتور وإن تابعه صاحب الكافي وكثير من المتأخرين فيه لأن قطع الجلد متحقق في الصورة الأولى منها لا سيما في الدامعة والدامية إذا الظاهر أن شيئاً من إظهار الدم وأصالته لا يتصور بدون قطع الجلد وقد صرح الشراح بتحقق قطع الجلد في كل الأنواع العشرة للشجة فكان التفسير المذكور شاملاً للكل غير مختص بالباضعة، فالظاهر في تفسير الباضعة هو ما ذكر في المحيط والبدائع حيث قال في المحيط ثم الباضعة وهي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه. وقال في البدائع والباضعة هي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه ا هـ. ويعضد ذلك ما وقع في معتبرات كتب اللغة قال في المغرب وفي الشجاج الباضعة وهي التي جرحت الجلد وشقت اللحم ا هـ . وقال في الصحاح : الباضعة الشجة التي تقطع الجلد وتشق اللحم وتدمي إلا أنها لا تسيل الدم. وقال في القاموس والباضعة الشجة التي تقطع الجلد وتشق اللحم شقاً خفيفاً وتدمي إلا أنها لا تسيل الدم ا هـ. لا يقال فعلى هذا يلزم تشبيه الباضعة بالمتلاحمة فإنهم قالوا والمتلاحمة هي التي تأخذ في اللحم وهذا في المآل غير ما نقلته عن قال شيخ الإسلام : ولا تنزع شيئاً من اللحم . ثم المتلاحمة وهي التي تقطع اللحم وتنزع شيئاً من اللحم؛ إلى هنا لفظ المحيط . وقال في البدائع : والباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه، والمتلاحمة التي تذهب في تتلاحم ولم تبلغ السمحاق ا هـ . وقال في القاموس : وشجة متلاحمة أخذت فيه ولم تبلغ السمحاق والمتلاحمة وهي التي تأخذ في اللحم كله ثم تتلاحم بعد ذلك أي تلتئم وتتلاصق سميت بذلك تفاؤلاً على ما يؤول إليه. وروي عن محمد أن المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة لأن المتلاحمة من قولهم التحم الشيئان إذا اتصل أحدهما بالآخر التي تظهر اللحم ولا تقطعه والباضعة بعدها لأنها تقطعه. وفي ظاهر الرواية والمتلاحمة تعمل في قطع أكثر اللحم وهي بعد الباضعة . وقال الأزهري : الأوجه أن يقال فالمتلاحمة هي قلنا من المعنى الظاهر هي
المتلاحمة أي القاطعة للحم والاختلاف الذي وجد في الشجاج راجع إلى مأخذ الاشتقاق لا إلى الحكم والسمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى السمحاق وهي الجلدة الرقيقة التي بين اللحم وعظم الرأس، والموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم أي تبينه، والهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، والمنقلة وهي التي تنقل العظم بعد الكسر أي تحوله والآمة وهي التي تصل إلى أم الدماغ، وأم الدماغ هي الجلدة الرقيقة التي تجمع الدماغ، وبعد الآمة شجة تسمى الدامغة بالغبن المعجمة وهي التي تصل إلى الدماغ لم يذكرها محمد لأن النفس لا تبقى بعدها عادة فتكون قتلاً، ولا تكون من الشجاج والكلام في الشجاج ولذا لم يذكر الخارصة والدامغة لأنها لا يبقى لها في الغالب أثر . وهذه الشجاج تختص بالرأس والوجه وما كان في غيرهما يسمى جراحة فهذا هو الحقيقة والحكم يترتب على الحقيقة فلا يجب بالجراحة ما يجب بالشجة من المقدار لأن التقدير بالنقل وهو إنما ورد في الشجاع وهي تختص بالرأس والوجه فخص الحكم المقدم بها، ولا يجوز إلحاق الجراحة بها دلالة ولا قياساً لأنها ليست في معناها في الشين لأن الوجه والرأس يظهران في الغالب وغيرهما مستور غالباً لا يظهر. واختلفوا في اللحيين فعندهما في الوجه فيتحقق الشجاج فيهما فيجب فيها موجبها خلافاً لما يقول مالك رحمه الله فإنه يقول إنهما ليسا من الوجه لأن المواجهة لا تقع بهما، ونحن نقول هما متصلان بالوجه من غير فاصل ويتحقق معنى المواجهة فصار كالذقن لأنهما تحتها. وقال شيخ الإسلام ويجب أن يفرض غسلهما في الوضوء لأنهما من الوجه حقيقة إلا أنا تركناهما للإجماع ولا إجماع هنا فبقينا العيرة للحقيقة .
وفي المبسوط : الشجاج في الرأس والوجه أحد عشر أولها الخارصة وهي تشق الجلد مأخوذة من قولهم خرص القصار الثوب إذا شقه من الدق ثم الدامعة وهي التي يخرج منها ما يشبه الدمع مأخوذة من دمع العين. ولم يذكرها محمد لأنها لم يبق لها أثر في الغالب، ثم التي يخرج منها الدم ثم الباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم، ثم المتلاحمة، وعن محمد أنه جعل المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة خلافاً لأبي يوسف وتفسيرها عند أبي يوسف التي تقشر الجلد وتجمع اللحم في موضع الجراحة ولا تقطعه مأخوذة من التحام؛ يقال التحم الجيشان إذا اجتمعا . ثم السمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى جلدة رقيقة فوق العظم تسمى السمحاق، ثم الموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم واللحم، ثم الهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، ثم المنقلة التي يخرج منها العظم لأنها تكسر العظم وتنقله عن موضعه، ثم الآمة التي تصل إلى أم الرأس وهي الجلدة التي فوق الدماغ، ثم الدامغة التي تخرق الجلد وتصل إلى الدماغ ولم يذكرها محمد لأن الإنسان لا يعيش معها. وأما أحكامها فإن كانت هذه الشجاج . ففي الموضحة القصاص لأن السكين ينتهي إلى العظم ولا يخاف منه الهلاك غالباً فيجب القصاص لقوله تعالى والجروح قصاص [المائدة : ٥] وذكر الكرخي عنه أنه ليس في شيء من عمداً
Book of Blood Money. It is: the eye, named so because blood flows from it like tears due to sparseness, or because one's eyes tear due to pain. In al-Muhit: ad-dami‘a is that from which something resembling tears flows, taken from tears; ad-damiya is that from which blood flows. Al-Marghinani mentioned that ad-damiya is that which bleeds without blood flowing—that is correct, related from Abu ‘Ubayd; ad-dami‘a is that from which blood flows like tears. Whoever says that the person's eyes tear from pain has gone far. Al-badi‘a is that which splits the skin, i.e., cuts it, taken from al-bad‘ meaning splitting and cutting, from which is the lancet. I say: the interpretation of al-badi‘a as mentioned by the commentator is weak, even if followed by the author of al-Kafi and many later scholars, because cutting the skin is realized in the first form, especially in ad-dami‘a and ad-damiya, since it is apparent that nothing of blood manifestation and essence is conceivable without cutting the skin. The commentators have explicitly stated the realization of cutting the skin in all ten types of head wound. So the mentioned interpretation covers all, not specific to al-badi‘a. The apparent interpretation of al-badi‘a is what is mentioned in al-Muhit and al-Bada’i‘: in al-Muhit, 'then al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it.' In al-Bada’i‘: 'al-badi‘a is that which cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it.' This is supported by reliable lexicons: in al-Mughrib: 'among head wounds, al-badi‘a is that which wounds the skin and splits the flesh.' In as-Sihah: 'al-badi‘a is the head wound that cuts the skin and splits the flesh and bleeds but does not cause blood to flow.' In al-Qamus: 'al-badi‘a is the head wound that cuts the skin and splits the flesh slightly and bleeds but does not cause blood to flow.' It cannot be said that this causes al-badi‘a to resemble al-mutalahima, because they said al-mutalahima is that which penetrates the flesh. This is ultimately not what I transmitted from al-Muhit and al-Bada’i‘ regarding the interpretation of al-badi‘a, for we say: the one who interprets al-badi‘a as such does not interpret al-mutalahima as mentioned such that confusion arises; rather, he adds a condition. For this, al-Muhit said: 'then al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it. Shaykh al-Islam said: it does not remove any flesh. Then al-mutalahima: it cuts the flesh and removes some flesh.' Up to here is the wording of al-Muhit. Al-Bada’i‘ said: 'al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it. Al-mutalahima: it goes into the flesh more than al-badi‘a does.' Al-Mughrib said: 'al-mutalahima among head wounds is that which splits the flesh without [reaching] the bone, then heals after splitting, i.e., becomes coherent.' As-Sihah said: 'al-mutalahima is the head wound that penetrates the flesh without the bone, then heals and does not reach as-simhaq.' Al-Qamus said: 'a mutalahima head wound: it penetrated the flesh and did not reach as-simhaq. Al-mutalahima is that which penetrates all the flesh then heals afterwards, i.e., closes and adheres; named so optimistically for its outcome.' It is related from Muhammad that al-mutalahima comes before al-badi‘a because al-mutalahima is from their saying 'the two things joined' when one connects with the other, which shows the flesh and does not cut it; al-badi‘a comes after because it cuts it. In the apparent narration (Zahir ar-Riwaya), al-mutalahima works in cutting more flesh and comes after al-badi‘a. Al-Azhari said: the most appropriate is to say al-mutalahima is, we say from the apparent meaning, it is the cutter of flesh. The difference found in head wounds refers to the derivation, not to the ruling. As-simhaq is that which reaches as-simhaq, which is the thin membrane between the flesh and the skull. Al-mudiha is that which exposes the bone, i.e., makes it apparent. Al-hashima is that which crushes the bone. Al-munaqqila is that which moves the bone after breakage, i.e., displaces it. Al-ammah is that which reaches the mother of the brain, and the mother of the brain is the thin membrane that contains the brain. After al-ammah there is a wound called ad-damigha (with a dotted ghayn) which reaches the brain; Muhammad did not mention it because life usually does not remain after it, so it is killing, and it is not among head wounds; the discussion is about head wounds. For this reason, he did not mention al-kharisa and ad-damigha because they usually leave no mark. These head wounds are specific to the head and face; what is elsewhere is called a wound (jarahah). This is the reality, and the ruling is based on reality. So what is obligatory for a head wound is not obligatory for a wound in terms of amount, because the determination is by transmitted texts, and that was only reported for head wounds, which are specific to the head and face, so the ruling is specific to them. It is not permissible to analogize wounds to them by implication or qiyas, because they are not similar in shamefulness, since the face and head are usually apparent, while others are usually covered, not apparent. They differed regarding the two jaws (al-lahyayn). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they are part of the face, so head wounds are realized on them, and their due is obligatory. Contrary to what Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) says, he says they are not part of the face because facing does not occur with them. We say: they are connected to the face without a separator and the meaning of facing is realized, so they become like the chin because they are below it. Shaykh al-Islam said: it would be obligatory to prescribe washing them in wudu' because they are actually part of the face, except we omitted it due to consensus; but here there is no consensus, so we adhere to the reality. In al-Mabsut: head wounds on the head and face are eleven. First: al-kharisa, which splits the skin, taken from their saying 'the fuller split the cloth when beating it.' Then ad-dami‘a, from which something resembling tears flows, taken from tears of the eye; Muhammad did not mention it because it usually leaves no effect. Then that from which blood flows. Then al-badi‘a, which cuts the flesh. Then al-mutalahima. From Muhammad that he placed al-mutalahima before al-badi‘a, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Its interpretation according to Abu Yusuf is that it peels the skin and collects the flesh at the site of the wound without cutting it, taken from iltiham; it is said 'the two armies joined' when they gathered. Then as-simhaq, which reaches a thin membrane above the bone called as-simhaq. Then al-mudiha, which exposes the bone and flesh. Then al-hashima, which crushes the bone. Then al-munaqqila, from which bone comes out, because it breaks the bone and moves it from its place. Then al-ammah, which reaches the mother of the head, which is the membrane above the brain. Then ad-damigha, which pierces the skin and reaches the brain; Muhammad did not mention it because a person does not live with it. As for their rulings: if these head wounds occur intentionally, then for al-mudiha there is qisas because the knife reaches the bone and death is not usually feared, so qisas is obligatory due to His statement: 'And wounds are subject to retaliation' (al-Ma'idah 5:45). Al-Karkhi mentioned from him that there is not in anything of intentional...

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the types of head wounds (shijaj) and their rulings. The author first defines various terms for wounds on the head and face, giving linguistic derivations and quoting earlier authorities like al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', and lexicons. He distinguishes between dami'a (blood flows like tears) and damiya (blood appears but does not flow), and explains the difference between badi'a (cuts flesh without removing any) and mutalahima (cuts and removes some flesh). He notes a difference of opinion between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the order of these two, with Imam Muhammad placing mutalahima before badi'a, but the Zahir al-Riwayah placing it after. The commentary clarifies that these differences are linguistic and do not affect the legal ruling. Next, the passage defines simhaq (reaches the membrane above bone), mudiha (exposes bone), hashima (crushes bone), munaqqila (displaces bone), ammah (reaches the brain membrane), and damigha (penetrates to brain, usually fatal). It emphasizes that head wounds (shijaj) are specific to the head and face; wounds elsewhere are called jarahah and are not subject to the fixed amounts set for shijaj. The author then discusses the inclusion of the jaws (lahyayn): according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, they are part of the face and thus shijaj apply, contrary to Malik. Finally, the passage states the ruling on qisas for mudiha: retaliation is obligatory because the knife reaches the bone and death is not typically feared, citing the Quranic verse 'and wounds are subject to retaliation'. The mention of al-Karkhi suggests a transmission that qisas is not applicable in some intentional cases, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
السمحاق (as-simhaq): The thin membrane between the flesh and the skull; also the wound that reaches it.
الموضحة (al-mudiha): A head wound that exposes the bone.
اللحيان (al-lahyan): The two jaws (bones of the lower face).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why are wounds on the head and face treated with fixed diya amounts while wounds elsewhere are not?
A) Because the head and face are more painful.
B) Because the head and face are usually visible, making the wound more shameful.
C) Because there is a hadith specifying only head wounds.
D) Because qiyas allows analogy from head to other body parts.

Key Takeaway
Shijaj rulings are confined to the head and face due to the nature of shamefulness (shayn), and the jaws are included in the face per the Hanafi school.
